# Input file: chapters/24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.claims.adoc

```
[[ch-24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec-claims]]
== Huitzilopochtli, Coyolxauhqui, and Coatepec — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Full chapter (CORE tier) on the Mexica cosmogonic-and-sovereignty
// combat in which Huitzilopochtli, born fully armed at the Mountain of
// Serpents (Coatepec), kills his elder sister Coyolxauhqui and his
// stellar brothers the Centzon Huitznahua at the moment of his birth,
// is the patron god of the Mexica state, and is annually re-enacted at
// the Templo Mayor of Tenochtitlan during the winter-solstice festival
// Panquetzaliztli. Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml, the
// chapter is single-prevalent (Florentine Codex Book 3 as the fullest
// single narrative; Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas and
// Durán as alternates) and targets approximately 9,000 narrative words.
// The chapter opens with Mexica framing — the state, the festival, the
// architecture — before any comparative invocation, per brief
// special_instructions and per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol".
// Area-specialist citation policy applies: Matos Moctezuma 1988,
// Carrasco 1999, and López Austin 1993 are cited by name in the first
// factual pass. The architectural-iconographic dimension (Templo
// Mayor, the Coyolxauhqui stone, the serpent-headed balustrades) is
// treated at the same level of attention as the textual narrative,
// per the brief's load-bearing claim that this is the most unambiguous
// single case of state-founded combat-cosmogony in the book.

=== Source overview

The chapter treats the Mexica cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat in which Huitzilopochtli is born fully armed from his mother Coatlicue at the Mountain of Serpents (Coatepec), and at the moment of his birth kills his elder sister Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua, his four hundred stellar brothers, who had come up the mountain to kill the pregnant Coatlicue and the not-yet-born god they regarded as a shame upon their lineage.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml; Matos Moctezuma 1988, *The Great Temple of the Aztecs: Treasures of Tenochtitlan* (London: Thames & Hudson; ISBN 9780500390245), interpretive chapters on Coatepec as Templo Mayor, on the Florentine Codex Book 3 narrative as the fullest single Coatepec account, and on the cosmogonic-and-sovereignty function of the combat within Mexica state religion.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Coatepec narrative has no pre-conquest written-text witness in the strict sense, since Mesoamerican writing-systems before 1521 CE were primarily pictographic-logographic and the Mexica did not record their narrative literature in continuous alphabetic prose; the surviving textual witnesses to the Coatepec story are post-conquest, written down by Nahua informants and Spanish friars in the generation after the fall of Tenochtitlan in 1521 CE.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, *City of Sacrifice: The Aztec Empire and the Role of Violence in Civilization* (Boston: Beacon Press; ISBN 9780807046425), chapter on Coatepec, on the post-conquest character of the surviving textual witnesses to the Coatepec narrative and on the absence of pre-conquest continuous-prose attestations of Mexica narrative literature; Matos Moctezuma 1988, on the same post-conquest textual situation; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml lacunae entry.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

[LACUNA: pre-conquest written-prose attestations of the Coatepec narrative are not available, and the surviving alphabetic-text witnesses are all post-conquest (1521 CE onward) compositions filtered through Spanish-language redaction and Spanish-friar conceptual frames; pre-conquest performance contexts (the Panquetzaliztli festival, the iconography at Templo Mayor) are partially recoverable through the post-conquest texts and through the archaeological record. | source: brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml lacunae entry; the Florentine Codex (Sahagún, 1545–1590 CE) is the principal post-conquest textual witness; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the post-conquest character of the surviving textual record. | scholarly_reconstruction: yes — corroborative material from the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas*, from Durán's *Historia de las Indias de Nueva España*, and from the archaeological evidence at Templo Mayor (the Coyolxauhqui stone, the serpent-headed balustrades, the structural layout of the double pyramid) supplies parallel witnesses to the post-conquest texts and is treated by Matos Moctezuma 1988 and Carrasco 1999 as material that confirms the broad outlines of the Coatepec narrative as preserved in the Florentine Codex Book 3.]

The fullest single textual witness to the Coatepec narrative is the *Historia general de las cosas de Nueva España* of the Franciscan friar Bernardino de Sahagún, compiled with Nahua informants between approximately 1545 and 1590 CE and preserved most fully in the bilingual Nahuatl-Spanish manuscript known as the *Florentine Codex* (held at the Biblioteca Medicea Laurenziana in Florence, MS Mediceo Palatino 218–220), whose Book 3 ("The Origin of the Gods") contains the fullest extant narrative account of Huitzilopochtli's birth at Coatepec and the killing of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on Coatepec as Templo Mayor, on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 ("The Origin of the Gods") as the fullest single textual witness to the Coatepec narrative and on its compilation by Sahagún with Nahua informants between c. 1545 and 1590 CE; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the *Florentine Codex* as the principal post-conquest textual witness for the Mexica narrative literature on Huitzilopochtli; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml lacunae entry naming Florentine Codex (Sahagún, 1545–1590) as the principal witness.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The *Florentine Codex* is a bilingual codex written in Classical Nahuatl in the left column and in sixteenth-century Spanish in the right column, with hundreds of pictorial illustrations executed by Nahua artists trained in the Franciscan school at Tlatelolco, and presents the Coatepec narrative as part of the wider encyclopaedic survey of pre-conquest Mexica religion, cosmology, history, and natural history that Sahagún and his collaborators undertook in the second half of the sixteenth century.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* as a bilingual Nahuatl-Spanish encyclopaedic codex compiled by Sahagún with Nahua collaborators trained at the Tlatelolco school, and on the pictorial illustrations executed by Nahua artists; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the encyclopaedic character of Sahagún's project and on the place of the Coatepec narrative within Book 3 ("The Origin of the Gods").]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The post-conquest textual witnesses to the Coatepec narrative include, alongside the *Florentine Codex* Book 3, the anonymous early-colonial Nahua-Spanish compilation *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* (compiled c. 1530s–1540s and preserving an earlier-stratum Mexica account with slightly different kinship details for the Centzon Huitznahua), the *Crónica Mexicáyotl* of Hernando Alvarado Tezozómoc (early seventeenth century), the *Anales de Cuauhtitlán* (sixteenth-century Nahuatl annals), and the *Historia de las Indias de Nueva España* of the Dominican friar Diego Durán (compiled c. 1581 and giving the Coatepec narrative its fullest ritual-context treatment).footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the post-conquest textual corpus for the Coatepec narrative including the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas*, the *Crónica Mexicáyotl*, the *Anales de Cuauhtitlán*, and Durán's *Historia*; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same corpus and on Durán's c. 1581 *Historia* as the fullest ritual-context source; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml lacunae entry on the corroborative material from these post-conquest sources.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The post-conquest written witnesses to the Coatepec narrative are filtered through Spanish-language redaction and Spanish-friar conceptual frames in ways that the chapter must register methodologically: Sahagún was a Catholic friar whose pastoral aim included the documentation of pre-conquest Mexica religion in order that Christian missionaries would be able to recognise and root out its survivals, and Durán was working from indigenous informants in a context in which Mexica narrative-religion was being actively suppressed; the chapter treats the post-conquest texts as load-bearing primary witnesses while flagging this filtering throughout.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the methodological situation of the post-conquest textual record and on the Spanish-friar conceptual frames through which Sahagún and Durán mediated their access to the pre-conquest Mexica narrative material; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the same methodological situation and on the corroborative role of the archaeological evidence at Templo Mayor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The chapter's archaeological-and-iconographic witness to the Coatepec narrative is the Templo Mayor of Tenochtitlan, the great double-stair pyramid at the centre of the Mexica capital whose excavations were resumed in 1978 CE following the chance discovery of the great Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of the southern (Huitzilopochtli) staircase, and whose architectural design and iconographic programme constitute a parallel witness to the Coatepec narrative that survives in the post-conquest texts.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Templo Mayor as the architectural-and-iconographic witness to the Coatepec narrative, on the resumption of the Templo Mayor excavations in 1978 CE under his own direction following the chance discovery of the Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of the southern staircase, and on the architectural-and-iconographic programme of the pyramid as a parallel witness to the post-conquest texts; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml variants.prevalent_version on the Templo Mayor archaeology as a parallel witness to the Florentine Codex Book 3.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the archaeological-iconographic dimension is Eduardo Matos Moctezuma's *The Great Temple of the Aztecs: Treasures of Tenochtitlan* (Thames & Hudson, 1988), the standard English-language interpretive monograph by the archaeologist who directed the Templo Mayor excavations from 1978 onward, and whose Coatepec-as-Templo-Mayor reading is the load-bearing scholarly argument that the architecture itself embodies the combat-narrative.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, *The Great Temple of the Aztecs: Treasures of Tenochtitlan* (London: Thames & Hudson, 1988; ISBN 9780500390245), as the standard English-language interpretive monograph on the Templo Mayor excavations and on the Coatepec-as-Templo-Mayor reading; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml sources.primary entry on Matos Moctezuma 1988 as the chapter's principal anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the political-and-ritual dimension is Davíd Carrasco's *City of Sacrifice: The Aztec Empire and the Role of Violence in Civilization* (Beacon Press, 1999), whose chapter on Coatepec supplies the standard English-language treatment of the political-ritual function of the combat-narrative within Mexica state ideology and of the Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival as the annual re-enactment context.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, *City of Sacrifice: The Aztec Empire and the Role of Violence in Civilization* (Boston: Beacon Press, 1999; ISBN 9780807046425), chapter on Coatepec as the chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the political-ritual dimension; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml sources.primary entry on Carrasco 1999.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the wider Mesoamerican comparative framework is Alfredo López Austin's *The Myths of the Opossum: Pathways of Mesoamerican Mythology* (University of New Mexico Press, 1993), whose chapter on cosmic combat places Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec within the wider Mesoamerican mythological landscape and supplies the chapter's working framework for distinguishing the combat-cosmogony of Coatepec from the dismemberment-without-combat cosmogony of Cipactli that the next family-chapter treats.footnote:[López Austin 1993, *The Myths of the Opossum: Pathways of Mesoamerican Mythology* (Albuquerque: University of New Mexico Press, 1993; ISBN 9780826313942), chapter on cosmic combat as the chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the Mesoamerican comparative framework; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml sources.primary entry on López Austin 1993.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

The chapter's secondary scholarly anchor for the wider non-Eurasian combat-myth comparison is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton University Press, 1987), whose treatment of non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels supplies a comparative anchor for the Coatepec narrative's place alongside Mesopotamian and other combat-myths in the long-arc combat-myth tradition that the present book surveys.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), treatment of non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels as a secondary anchor for the present chapter; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml sources.secondary entry on Forsyth 1987 on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels

The chapter is a CORE-tier full-chapter treatment with a target of approximately 9,000 narrative words, organising the Coatepec narrative as a connected story across the principal post-conquest textual witnesses (with the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 as the prevalent version) and presenting the architectural-and-iconographic evidence at the Templo Mayor (the structural layout of the double pyramid, the Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of the southern staircase, the serpent-headed balustrades, the Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival as annual re-enactment) at the same level of attention as the textual narrative itself.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml on the CORE-tier classification, the 9,000-word target, the single-prevalent variant classification with the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 as the prevalent version, and the special_instructions on equal-attention treatment of the architectural-iconographic dimension; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the architecture-embodies-the-narrative reading; scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 10 on the Americas placement of the Huitzilopochtli chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter's framing premise is that the Coatepec narrative supplies one of the four clearest cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat cases across non-cognate traditions for the present book's Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony), alongside Marduk-Tiamat in the *Enūma Eliš*, Ninurta-Asag in *Lugal-e*, and Gonggong's broken pillar — and is unparalleled within the book in the explicitness of its architectural and ritual embedding, since the narrative is not merely told and re-enacted but is built in stone at the political centre of the Mexica state.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks on Part II C1's pairing of Huitzilopochtli with Marduk, Ninurta, and Gonggong as the four strongest independent attestations of the cosmogonic-combat subtype; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the architectural-embedding of the Coatepec narrative at the Templo Mayor as unparalleled in explicitness; scope.md §"Book structure" Part II on the C1 chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

=== The Mexica state, the festival, and the architecture

The Mexica were the dominant Nahuatl-speaking polity of the Basin of Mexico in the late pre-conquest period (c. 1325–1521 CE), with their capital at Tenochtitlan (founded c. 1325 CE on an island in Lake Texcoco) and their tributary empire extending across central Mexico from the Pacific coast in the west to the Gulf coast in the east at its greatest extent under the rulers Moctezuma I, Axayácatl, Ahuítzotl, and Moctezuma II.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Mexica polity of the Basin of Mexico, on Tenochtitlan as its capital founded c. 1325 CE on an island in Lake Texcoco, and on the tributary empire's greatest extent under the late pre-conquest rulers; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Mexica imperial context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

Huitzilopochtli — whose name is conventionally glossed in modern scholarship as "Hummingbird-of-the-South" or "Hummingbird-on-the-Left" — is the patron god of the Mexica state, the sun-warrior whose daily rising in the east is read in the Mexica theological tradition as the daily re-enactment of his Coatepec victory over his lunar sister and his stellar brothers, and whose tutelary relationship to the Mexica polity is co-constitutive with the political identity of the state itself.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on Huitzilopochtli as patron god of the Mexica state, on the conventional gloss of his name as "Hummingbird-of-the-South" / "Hummingbird-on-the-Left", and on the daily-rising solar reading of the Coatepec victory; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the co-constitutive relation between Huitzilopochtli's tutelary role and the political identity of the Mexica state; glossary 'Huitzilopochtli' first_mention_gloss.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Templo Mayor, the great double-stair pyramid at the geographical and ritual centre of Tenochtitlan, was the principal sanctuary of Huitzilopochtli — the southern (left-hand) summit-shrine on the pyramid's twin platforms was dedicated to the god, with the northern (right-hand) summit-shrine dedicated to the rain-god Tlaloc — and the structure was rebuilt and enlarged at least seven times over the polity's two-century lifespan, with each successive enlargement encasing the earlier structure within the new one in a stratigraphic sequence that the 1978-onward excavations have recovered.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Templo Mayor as the principal sanctuary of Huitzilopochtli, on the southern Huitzilopochtli summit-shrine paired with the northern Tlaloc summit-shrine on the pyramid's double-stair platform, on the seven principal building stages identified by the excavations, and on the stratigraphic sequence of nested structures recovered from 1978 onward.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Templo Mayor's architectural identification as Coatepec — the Mountain of Serpents on which Huitzilopochtli is born and fights — is the load-bearing scholarly argument of Matos Moctezuma 1988 and is supported by multiple converging lines of iconographic and architectural evidence: the great serpent-headed balustrades flanking the southern (Huitzilopochtli) staircase, which give the structure its character as a serpent-mountain; the great Coyolxauhqui stone discovered in 1978 at the foot of that staircase, which depicts the dismembered moon-goddess at the base of Coatepec; and the structural design of the double pyramid as a four-tiered cosmic mountain with the god's shrine at its summit.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Templo Mayor as Coatepec, on the converging lines of evidence (the serpent-headed balustrades flanking the southern staircase, the great Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of that staircase, and the four-tiered cosmic-mountain structural design) supporting the architectural identification; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same architectural identification.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The great Coyolxauhqui stone — a roughly circular monolith approximately 3.25 metres in diameter, sculpted in low relief on its upper face — was discovered on 21 February 1978 by electrical-utility workers digging a trench at the corner of Calle Guatemala and Calle de la Argentina in central Mexico City, lies at the foot of the southern (Huitzilopochtli) staircase of the Stage IV phase of the Templo Mayor, and depicts the moon-goddess in the moment of her dismemberment, with her head, arms, and legs severed and arranged around her nude torso, the bells of her cheeks visible on her face.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Coyolxauhqui stone (approximately 3.25 metres in diameter) discovered on 21 February 1978 at the corner of Calle Guatemala and Calle de la Argentina in central Mexico City, on its placement at the foot of the southern (Huitzilopochtli) staircase of the Stage IV Templo Mayor, and on its iconographic depiction of the dismembered moon-goddess with the bells on her cheeks visible; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same iconography.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Coyolxauhqui stone's placement at the foot of the southern staircase is iconographically and ritually consequential: the stone depicts the dismembered moon-goddess at the base of the cosmic mountain Coatepec, and the bodies of sacrificial victims cast down the staircase from the summit-shrine of Huitzilopochtli at the top of the pyramid would have come to rest on or beside the stone, completing the architectural-and-ritual re-enactment of the moment in the narrative when the newly born god flings the body of his sister down the side of the mountain.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the architectural-and-ritual re-enactment in which sacrificial victims cast down the southern staircase from the Huitzilopochtli summit-shrine come to rest on or beside the Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of the staircase, completing the re-enactment of the goddess's dismemberment at the base of Coatepec; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same architectural-and-ritual re-enactment.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The Panquetzaliztli ("Raising of Banners") festival, the Mexica winter-solstice festival held in the corresponding twenty-day veintena of the eighteen-month Mexica calendrical year (the European-calendar dates fall around the winter solstice in late November to mid-December in the Julian system used in the early colonial sources, with the precise correspondence varying with the Mexica calendrical drift), was the annual ritual context in which the Coatepec narrative was publicly re-enacted at the Templo Mayor, and was one of the two principal annual festivals of the Mexica state ceremonial calendar.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Panquetzaliztli ("Raising of Banners") winter-solstice festival as the annual ritual context in which the Coatepec narrative was re-enacted at the Templo Mayor and as one of the two principal annual festivals of the Mexica state ceremonial calendar, on its placement in the corresponding twenty-day veintena of the Mexica calendrical year, and on the Julian-calendar date-correspondence in the early colonial sources; glossary 'Panquetzaliztli' first_mention_gloss.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The Panquetzaliztli festival included a sequence of public ritual elements that re-enacted components of the Coatepec narrative: the fashioning of a dough-image (*tzoalli*) of Huitzilopochtli that was processed through the city; mock combats between pairs of warriors representing Huitzilopochtli's forces and the Centzon Huitznahua; the casting of sacrificial victims' bodies down the southern staircase of the Templo Mayor onto the Coyolxauhqui stone; and the ritual feasting on portions of the dough-image of the god himself by the assembled celebrants, in a re-enactment-and-communion sequence whose structural-symbolic logic Carrasco 1999 reads as the ritual armature of the chapter's narrative material.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Panquetzaliztli festival's ritual sequence including the dough-image (*tzoalli*) of Huitzilopochtli processed through the city, the mock combats between pairs of warriors representing Huitzilopochtli's forces and the Centzon Huitznahua, the casting of sacrificial victims onto the Coyolxauhqui stone, and the ritual feasting on the dough-image; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same festival sequence.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The Mexica practice of human sacrifice at the Templo Mayor — chiefly of war-captives taken in the so-called "flowery wars" (*xōchiyāōyōtl*) waged against neighbouring polities such as Tlaxcala — is interpreted in Carrasco 1999's reading as the ritual performance of the Coatepec narrative on a cosmic-political scale, with the prisoners' deaths and the casting of their bodies down the staircase functioning as the perpetual feeding of Huitzilopochtli's solar-warrior energy and as the perpetual re-enactment of the moment when the newly born god dismembers his lunar-stellar siblings.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Mexica practice of human sacrifice at the Templo Mayor as the ritual performance of the Coatepec narrative on a cosmic-political scale, on the principal source of war-captives in the "flowery wars" (*xōchiyāōyōtl*) waged against neighbouring polities such as Tlaxcala, and on the ritual logic of the casting of bodies down the southern staircase as perpetual re-enactment of the dismemberment of the lunar-stellar siblings; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same ritual logic.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

[INFERENCE: the chapter reads the Templo Mayor architecture, the Coyolxauhqui stone, the Panquetzaliztli festival, and the Mexica sacrificial practice as four converging witnesses to a single integrated ritual-architectural-iconographic-narrative complex centred on the Coatepec narrative — that is, as four faces of a single thing rather than as four loosely related practices. | basis: Matos Moctezuma 1988 treats the Templo Mayor architecture and the Coyolxauhqui stone as a single architectural-iconographic statement of the Coatepec narrative, and Carrasco 1999 reads the Panquetzaliztli festival and the sacrificial practice as the ritual performance of the same narrative on the same architectural stage; the four converging witnesses are independently attested in different lines of evidence (architecture, sculpture, ritual calendar, sacrificial practice) and converge on the same Coatepec narrative as their referent. | risk: a stronger reading would treat the four witnesses as separately constituted ritual-political phenomena that converge only contingently on the Coatepec narrative; the chapter's integrated-complex reading is the standard scholarly synthesis of Matos Moctezuma 1988 and Carrasco 1999 but represents a synthesis rather than a single source-attested formulation.]

=== The Coatepec narrative: Coatlicue and the conception of Huitzilopochtli

The Coatepec narrative as preserved in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 opens with the goddess Coatlicue ("She-of-the-Serpent-Skirt"), the earth-goddess mother of Huitzilopochtli, sweeping at the holy mountain Coatepec ("Mountain of Serpents") near Tula in the pre-Mexica era, in the company of her daughter Coyolxauhqui and her four hundred sons the Centzon Huitznahua.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative opening with Coatlicue ("She-of-the-Serpent-Skirt") sweeping at the holy mountain Coatepec ("Mountain of Serpents") near Tula in the pre-Mexica era, in the company of her daughter Coyolxauhqui and her four hundred sons the Centzon Huitznahua; glossary 'Coatlicue' / 'Coatepec' / 'Coyolxauhqui' / 'Centzon Huitznahua' first_mention_gloss entries.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

Coatlicue's iconographic identity is monumentally captured in the great Coatlicue statue in the Museo Nacional de Antropología in Mexico City, an Aztec-period basalt sculpture approximately 2.6 metres tall that depicts the goddess with a skirt of intertwined serpents, a necklace of human hands and hearts, two outward-facing serpent-heads as her own face (formed where her decapitated head would otherwise be), and clawed feet — the iconography supplying a parallel non-textual witness to her character as earth-and-death-and-fertility goddess.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the great Coatlicue statue in the Museo Nacional de Antropología in Mexico City, an Aztec-period basalt sculpture approximately 2.6 metres tall depicting the goddess with serpent-skirt, necklace of human hands and hearts, twin outward-facing serpent-heads in place of her decapitated head, and clawed feet, as a parallel non-textual witness to her identity as earth-and-death-and-fertility goddess; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same iconography.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

Coatlicue's conception of Huitzilopochtli is, in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative, miraculous and non-sexual: as she is sweeping at Coatepec, a ball of feathers (in some translations "a small bundle of feathers" or "a tuft of feathers") descends from the sky and Coatlicue places it in her bosom, intending to keep it; afterward, when she searches for the feather-ball, she cannot find it; from that moment she is pregnant, with the feather-ball having entered her womb in a virgin-conception event.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Coatlicue's miraculous conception of Huitzilopochtli through a ball of feathers (variously translated "a small bundle of feathers" or "a tuft of feathers") that descends from the sky as she is sweeping at Coatepec, that she places in her bosom intending to keep, that she cannot find when she searches for it, and that has entered her womb in a virgin-conception event; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same conception narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The feather-ball as the agent of Coatlicue's conception is iconographically suggestive: Huitzilopochtli's name "Hummingbird-of-the-South" links the god to the iridescent feathers of the hummingbird, and the feather-fall as the moment of conception is read in the Mexica theological tradition as the descent of a sky-element (the feather as solar-bird-token) into the earth-goddess's body, with the resulting union producing the sun-warrior god whose own identity is that of the descending solar element born from the earth-mother.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the iconographic suggestiveness of the feather-ball conception as the descent of a solar-bird-token (the hummingbird-feather) into the earth-goddess's body and as the theological logic of the sun-warrior god born from the earth-mother in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same theological logic; glossary 'Huitzilopochtli' first_mention_gloss on the "Hummingbird-of-the-South" gloss.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

Coyolxauhqui ("Bells-on-Her-Cheeks") and the Centzon Huitznahua ("Four Hundred Southerners"), Coatlicue's daughter and her four hundred sons by an earlier (and unspecified) progeny, perceive the unexplained pregnancy as a shame upon their lineage — the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative emphasises that they take their mother's pregnancy as an outrage demanding vengeance — and Coyolxauhqui leads the Centzon Huitznahua in a plot to kill their mother before the unborn god can be born.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Coyolxauhqui ("Bells-on-Her-Cheeks") and the Centzon Huitznahua ("Four Hundred Southerners") perceiving Coatlicue's unexplained pregnancy as a shame upon their lineage and on Coyolxauhqui's leadership of the plot to kill their mother before the unborn god can be born; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same conspiracy narrative; glossary 'Coyolxauhqui' / 'Centzon Huitznahua' first_mention_gloss entries.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The number four hundred (*centzontli*) attached to the Centzon Huitznahua is in Classical Nahuatl a stock collective number for "many" or "innumerable" (the same number of southern brothers is paralleled by the *centzon mimixcoa* of the northern stars in other Mexica narratives), and the Centzon Huitznahua are theologically identified with the southern stars (and, by extension, with the night sky as the field of the lunar-stellar order over which the dawning sun must triumph each morning).footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the number four hundred (*centzontli*) as a stock Classical Nahuatl collective number for "many" or "innumerable", on the parallel *centzon mimixcoa* of the northern stars in other Mexica narratives, and on the theological identification of the Centzon Huitznahua with the southern stars and the night-sky lunar-stellar order; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same theological identification.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

[VARIANT: primary=*Florentine Codex* Book 3 (Sahagún 1545–1590; Anderson-Dibble English translation): Coatlicue is impregnated by a ball of feathers as she sweeps at Coatepec; Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua plot to kill her; one of the brothers (Cuahuitlicac) reveals the plot to the unborn Huitzilopochtli; Huitzilopochtli is born fully armed with the *xiuhcōātl* turquoise-serpent weapon and kills Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua at the moment of his birth | alt=*Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* (anonymous c. 1530s–1540s): a slightly different emphasis with variant kinship details for the Centzon Huitznahua (in some passages identified differently in their relation to Coatlicue); Durán's *Historia de las Indias de Nueva España* (c. 1581): a fuller ritual context for the narrative, treating it as closely tied to the Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival and supplying additional ritual-aetiological elements that the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 omits | chosen: A (*Florentine Codex* Book 3 as the prevalent version) | reason: per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml variants.classification = single-prevalent; the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 is the fullest single textual narrative of the Coatepec story, is corroborated by the other sixteenth-century Nahua / Spanish sources, and is paralleled by the archaeological evidence at the Templo Mayor (the Coyolxauhqui stone, the serpent-headed balustrades, the structural layout); the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* and Durán's *Historia* are cited in their respective sub-claims for the kinship-detail and ritual-context variants while the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 supplies the chapter's main narrative skeleton.]

The conspiracy is in some strata of the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative betrayed to the unborn god by one of his own brothers — Cuahuitlicac, who turns informer and reveals to the not-yet-born Huitzilopochtli inside Coatlicue's womb the details of the conspiracy as they unfold (where the Centzon Huitznahua are gathering, what their armour and weapons are, what their plan of attack will be), supplying the unborn god with continuous intelligence so that he is forewarned at each stage of the approach.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Cuahuitlicac as the brother who turns informer and reveals to the unborn Huitzilopochtli inside Coatlicue's womb the details of the Centzon Huitznahua conspiracy as they unfold, supplying continuous intelligence on their gathering, armour, weapons, and plan of attack; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same Cuahuitlicac informer-narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

Coatlicue herself, in some strata of the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative, is afraid as the conspiracy approaches the holy mountain — the conspirators arrive at the foot of Coatepec armed for battle, with Coyolxauhqui at their head — but the unborn god speaks from within her womb, telling her not to fear, that he will defend her, and that he is ready, the colloquy between mother and unborn god being one of the narrative's most distinctive textual features.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Coatlicue's fear as the conspirators (with Coyolxauhqui at their head) arrive at the foot of Coatepec armed for battle, on the unborn god's reassurance from within her womb, and on the colloquy between mother and unborn god as a distinctive textual feature of the narrative; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same colloquy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

=== The combat at Coatepec

The combat at Coatepec begins, in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative, at the moment Huitzilopochtli is born — fully grown, fully armed, and equipped for war — emerging from Coatlicue's womb at the summit of the holy mountain just as Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua reach the top, with the timing of the birth being itself the narrative's first decisive movement against the conspirators.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Huitzilopochtli's birth at Coatepec, on his emergence fully grown, fully armed, and equipped for war from Coatlicue's womb at the moment Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua reach the summit, and on the timing of the birth as the narrative's first decisive movement against the conspirators; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same birth-as-combat-opening.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

Huitzilopochtli's birth-arms include the *xiuhcōātl* (the turquoise-serpent weapon — a fire-serpent of solar character whose iconographic appearance in Mexica art is a serpent-shaped firebrand or fire-spear with a recurved snout) which the newly born god wields as his decisive weapon against Coyolxauhqui, and a body-paint and feathered war-regalia which the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative describes in detail at the moment of his appearance at the mountain's summit.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on Huitzilopochtli's birth-arms in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative, on the *xiuhcōātl* (turquoise-serpent / fire-serpent weapon) as a serpent-shaped firebrand or fire-spear with recurved snout in the Mexica iconographic tradition, on its decisive role in the killing of Coyolxauhqui, and on the body-paint and feathered war-regalia described in the narrative; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same iconography.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The fully-armed-from-birth pattern is theologically load-bearing for the chapter's reading of Huitzilopochtli as a sun-warrior whose combat with Coyolxauhqui is enacted at the moment of his coming-into-being rather than at a later mature-stage moment of his career: the god's identity and the combat are inseparable, since the very emergence of the sun-warrior IS the killing of the lunar-stellar siblings, with no narrative gap between birth and combat in which the god might be characterised independently of the killing.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the theological load-bearing of the fully-armed-from-birth pattern for the reading of Huitzilopochtli as a sun-warrior whose identity is inseparable from the combat, on the simultaneity of his coming-into-being with the killing of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua, and on the absence of a narrative gap between birth and combat in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 account; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same theological reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The killing of Coyolxauhqui is, in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative, immediate and total: the newly born Huitzilopochtli strikes off Coyolxauhqui's head with the *xiuhcōātl* at the summit of Coatepec, then dismembers her body — severing her arms and legs from her torso — and rolls or flings the dismembered body down the side of the holy mountain so that it comes to rest at the foot of Coatepec, with the dismemberment being the most iconographically explicit moment of the narrative.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Huitzilopochtli's killing of Coyolxauhqui as an immediate and total dismemberment at the summit of Coatepec, on the use of the *xiuhcōātl* to strike off her head, on the severing of her arms and legs from her torso, and on the rolling or flinging of the dismembered body down the side of the holy mountain; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same dismemberment narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Coyolxauhqui stone at the foot of the southern Templo Mayor staircase is the architectural-iconographic re-enactment of this exact moment of the narrative: the stone depicts the dismembered moon-goddess at the base of Coatepec, head severed, arms and legs disarticulated and arranged radially around her nude torso, and is positioned on the ground at the foot of the staircase up which the worshippers and sacrificial victims would have processed toward the summit-shrine of Huitzilopochtli — the stone is the narrative-moment frozen in basalt at the precise location where the narrative-moment occurs.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the Coyolxauhqui stone as the architectural-iconographic re-enactment of the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 dismemberment-moment, on its iconographic depiction of the moon-goddess with severed head and disarticulated limbs arranged radially around her nude torso, and on its placement at the foot of the southern staircase as the precise location at which the narrative-moment occurs in the architectural re-enactment; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same architectural-iconographic logic.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The killing of the Centzon Huitznahua follows in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative immediately after the dismemberment of Coyolxauhqui: Huitzilopochtli pursues the four hundred southern stellar brothers across the mountain, around its slopes, and (in some strata of the narrative) four times in succession around the summit, killing them as they flee — the chase imagery being the load-bearing narrative figure for the daily-rising-sun-pursuing-and-extinguishing-the-stars cosmological reading of the combat.footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Huitzilopochtli's pursuit of the Centzon Huitznahua across the slopes of Coatepec following the dismemberment of Coyolxauhqui, on the four-times-around-the-summit chase pattern in some strata of the narrative, and on the chase imagery as the load-bearing figure for the daily-rising-sun-pursuing-and-extinguishing-the-stars cosmological reading; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same chase narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

A small remnant of the Centzon Huitznahua is in some strata of the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative permitted to escape — fleeing south to a region whose name is rendered in some translations as Huitzlampa ("place of thorns" or "south") — and the survival of this remnant as the visible southern stars in the night sky is the narrative's aetiology for the persistence of stars in the Mexica heaven despite the daily victory of the sun-warrior god.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative-stratum in which a small remnant of the Centzon Huitznahua is permitted to escape, fleeing south to a region named in some translations as Huitzlampa ("place of thorns" or "south"), and on the aetiological reading of this remnant as the visible southern stars in the night sky despite the daily victory of the sun-warrior god; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same aetiological reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The combat at Coatepec is therefore in the Mexica theological reading not a one-time foundational combat that establishes the cosmos and recedes into the past, but a daily-recurring cosmic event whose primordial-narrative form (Huitzilopochtli's birth at Coatepec) is the perpetual archetype of the daily-recurring fact (the sun's rising in the east, the moon's setting, the stars' fading) — the combat is inscribed in the daily course of the sky, with each dawn re-enacting the killing of Coyolxauhqui and the pursuit of the Centzon Huitznahua.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Mexica theological reading of the combat at Coatepec as a daily-recurring cosmic event whose primordial-narrative form is the perpetual archetype of the daily fact of the sun's rising and the moon's and stars' fading, and on the inscription of the combat in the daily course of the sky as continuous re-enactment; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same daily-cosmic reading; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cultural_relevance.function entry on the cosmological function: "the diurnal solar-kingship of Huitzilopochtli over the defeated moon (Coyolxauhqui) and stars (Centzon Huitznahua) is performed every day: the sun's rising IS the victory".]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

=== The cosmogonic and sovereignty work of the combat

The combat at Coatepec performs simultaneous cosmogonic and sovereignty work in the Mexica theological reading: the establishment of the proper order of the Mexica cosmos (sun-and-day above, moon-and-stars-and-night below; solar-warrior-god as the ruling celestial body) is the cosmogonic outcome of the combat, while the establishment of Huitzilopochtli's sovereignty over his lunar-and-stellar siblings — and, by extension, over the wider Mexica pantheon — is the sovereignty outcome, with the two functions inseparable in the Mexica theological tradition.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the simultaneous cosmogonic and sovereignty work of the combat at Coatepec, on the establishment of the proper order of the Mexica cosmos as the cosmogonic outcome and on the establishment of Huitzilopochtli's sovereignty over his lunar-and-stellar siblings and the wider Mexica pantheon as the sovereignty outcome; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same dual function; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cultural_relevance.function entries on royal-legitimation and cosmological functions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The Mexica state's claim to the Coatepec narrative as its founding combat is explicit in the political-ritual record: Tenochtitlan as the political centre of the Mexica polity is architecturally identified with Coatepec via the Templo Mayor at its centre, and the Mexica royal-legitimation narrative traces the polity's authority to the patron god whose Coatepec victory established his sovereignty and (by extension) the sovereignty of the Mexica state that he favours.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Mexica state's explicit claim to the Coatepec narrative as its founding combat, on Tenochtitlan as architecturally identified with Coatepec via the Templo Mayor at its centre, and on the Mexica royal-legitimation narrative that traces the polity's authority to the patron god's Coatepec victory; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same political-ritual claim; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cultural_relevance.function royal-legitimation entry on Tenochtitlan's political centre as architecturally the cosmic mountain of the god's victory.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The architectural-embedding of the Coatepec narrative at the political centre of the Mexica state is unparalleled in the present book in its explicitness: in no other chaoskampf treated in this book is the combat-narrative literally built in stone at the political-ritual centre of the relevant polity, with the architecture itself functioning as the primary statement of the combat-cosmogony rather than as a backdrop to a separately textual narrative.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks entry on the architectural-embedded combat (Templo Mayor = Coatepec) as unparalleled in explicitness — no other chaoskampf in the book has its narrative literally built in stone at state-political centre; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same architectural-embedding reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The Mexica practice of warfare and human sacrifice is in Carrasco 1999's reading the perpetual prosecution of the Coatepec combat at the political-ritual scale: the polity's military expansion against neighbouring polities is the field of the daily reproduction of the combat, the war-captives are the brothers-and-sister Huitzilopochtli must continually kill, and the casting of their bodies down the southern staircase of the Templo Mayor is the architectural-iconographic completion of the killing-of-Coyolxauhqui in real time at the political-ritual centre.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the Mexica practice of warfare and human sacrifice as the perpetual prosecution of the Coatepec combat at the political-ritual scale, on the military expansion against neighbouring polities as the field of the daily reproduction of the combat, on the war-captives as the brothers-and-sister Huitzilopochtli must continually kill, and on the casting of bodies down the southern staircase as the architectural-iconographic completion of the killing-of-Coyolxauhqui at the political-ritual centre; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same political-ritual logic.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of the Coatepec narrative as the most unambiguous single case of state-founded combat-cosmogony in the present book — i.e., as the case in which the integration of cosmic-narrative, ritual, architecture, and state-political identity is most complete — is an editorial synthesis of the brief's framing and of the converging Matos Moctezuma 1988 and Carrasco 1999 readings, presented as a chapter-level claim rather than as a single source-attested formulation. | basis: Matos Moctezuma 1988's architectural reading of the Templo Mayor as Coatepec, Carrasco 1999's political-ritual reading of the Panquetzaliztli festival and the Mexica sacrificial practice as the ritual performance of the Coatepec narrative, and the brief's special_instructions claim that this is "the most unambiguous single case of state-founded combat-cosmogony in the book". | risk: comparative judgements of the form "the most unambiguous single case" are necessarily relative to the comparison-set and to the analyst's reading-criteria; alternative readings would prefer Marduk-Tiamat (where the ritual context of the Akitu festival has its own strong claim) or other cases; the chapter's "most unambiguous" reading is presented as a defensible chapter-level synthesis rather than as a knockdown comparative claim, and Part II C1 of the present book will develop the comparative argument across the four cosmogonic-combat cases.]

The Coatepec narrative's place within the Mesoamerican mythological landscape is, in López Austin 1993's wider framework, distinctive: where most Mesoamerican cosmogonies known from the post-conquest sources rely on a dismemberment-without-combat structure (the primordial earth-monster Cipactli torn apart by Quetzalcoatl and Tezcatlipoca to form sky and earth, treated in the next chapter), the Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec narrative is one of the principal Mesoamerican attestations of a combat-cosmogony in which the cosmic order is established through and as a consequence of an actual combat between divine adversaries.footnote:[López Austin 1993, *The Myths of the Opossum*, chapter on cosmic combat, on the place of the Huitzilopochtli-at-Coatepec narrative within the wider Mesoamerican mythological landscape, on the contrast with the dismemberment-without-combat structure of the Cipactli cosmogony (Quetzalcoatl and Tezcatlipoca tearing apart the primordial earth-monster to form sky and earth) treated in the next chapter, and on the Coatepec narrative as one of the principal Mesoamerican attestations of a combat-cosmogony.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

The Mesoamerican distinction between combat-cosmogony (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec) and dismemberment-without-combat cosmogony (Cipactli torn apart by Quetzalcoatl and Tezcatlipoca) marks the present book's boundary between in-scope chaoskampf and out-of-scope dismemberment-cosmogony per scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" — and the Mesoamerican tradition itself contains both types of cosmogony, supplying a within-tradition contrast that the next family-chapter on other Mesoamerican combats develops.footnote:[López Austin 1993, *The Myths of the Opossum*, chapter on cosmic combat, on the Mesoamerican distinction between combat-cosmogony and dismemberment-without-combat cosmogony as the present book's boundary case at scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs"; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks entry on the Cipactli dismemberment-cosmogony marking the book's boundary; scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" 1 on dismemberment cosmogonies without combat (Ymir, Puruṣa, Pangu, arguably Cipactli) as out-of-scope.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

The Mexica narrative of Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec is therefore — in López Austin 1993's wider framework and in the present chapter's typological reading — a combat-cosmogony in the strong scope.md §"Subject definition" sense: the cosmos's present features (the sun's regnant relation to the moon and stars; the day's rising over the night) emerge from the combat's outcome rather than from a pre-existing arrangement that the combat merely preserves, and the narrative is therefore one of the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases for Part II C1.footnote:[López Austin 1993, *The Myths of the Opossum*, chapter on cosmic combat, on the Coatepec narrative as a combat-cosmogony in the strong sense — the cosmos's present features emerge from the combat's outcome rather than from a pre-existing arrangement; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml on the chapter's role as one of the four clearest cosmogonic-combat cases for Part II C1; scope.md §"Subject definition" 1 on the cosmogonic chaoskampf subtype.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

=== Variants in the kinship and ritual contexts

The *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* — an anonymous early-colonial Nahua-Spanish compilation, often attributed to Andrés de Olmos and dated to the 1530s–1540s, that preserves an earlier-stratum Mexica account drawn from indigenous pictorial sources — supplies a slightly different version of the Coatepec narrative in which the kinship details for the Centzon Huitznahua differ in some passages from those of the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 (their relation to Coatlicue and to Coyolxauhqui being characterised differently in the *Historia*'s account), without affecting the central combat-narrative as preserved in the *Florentine Codex*.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* (anonymous, often attributed to Andrés de Olmos, c. 1530s–1540s) as preserving an earlier-stratum Mexica account with variant kinship details for the Centzon Huitznahua differing in some passages from the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 account, without affecting the central combat-narrative; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same variant; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml variants.alternates entry on the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas* difference.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

Diego Durán's *Historia de las Indias de Nueva España* (compiled c. 1581 from Nahua informants and earlier Mexica pictorial sources, preserved in the manuscript at the Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid) supplies the Coatepec narrative within an unusually full ritual-context apparatus that ties the narrative closely to the Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival, supplying ritual-aetiological elements (the dough-image of the god, the mock combats of the festival, the casting of victims down the staircase) that the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 omits or treats more cursorily.footnote:[López Austin 1993, *The Myths of the Opossum*, chapter on cosmic combat, on Durán's *Historia de las Indias de Nueva España* (c. 1581; preserved at the Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid) as supplying the Coatepec narrative within an unusually full ritual-context apparatus that ties the narrative closely to the Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival and that supplies ritual-aetiological elements (dough-image, mock combats, casting of victims down the staircase) the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 omits or treats cursorily; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml variants.alternates entry on Durán's fuller ritual context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

The *Crónica Mexicáyotl* of Hernando Alvarado Tezozómoc (a Mexica nobleman writing in the early seventeenth century, preserving Mexica dynastic-and-mythological tradition in a continuous Nahuatl-language chronicle) and the *Anales de Cuauhtitlán* (sixteenth-century Nahuatl annals from the Valley of Mexico) preserve further Mexica-tradition material on Huitzilopochtli that is broadly consistent with the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative while supplying additional historical-and-mythological context (notably the wandering of the Mexica from Aztlán under the patronage of Huitzilopochtli, which precedes the Coatepec narrative in the wider Mexica tradition).footnote:[Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the *Crónica Mexicáyotl* of Hernando Alvarado Tezozómoc (early seventeenth century) and the *Anales de Cuauhtitlán* (sixteenth-century Nahuatl annals) as preserving further Mexica-tradition material on Huitzilopochtli broadly consistent with the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative and supplying additional historical-and-mythological context, including the Mexica wandering from Aztlán under the patronage of Huitzilopochtli; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same wider tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter takes the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 as the prevalent narrative version per the brief's variant classification and presents the *Historia de los Mexicanos por sus pinturas*' kinship-detail variants and Durán's ritual-context fuller treatment as alternates that supplement rather than displace the prevalent version, with the archaeological evidence at the Templo Mayor (the Coyolxauhqui stone, the serpent-headed balustrades, the structural layout) functioning as a parallel non-textual witness that confirms the broad outlines of the prevalent version.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml variants.classification = single-prevalent with the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 as the prevalent version corroborated by the other sixteenth-century Nahua / Spanish sources and by the archaeological evidence at the Templo Mayor; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the parallel-non-textual-witness role of the archaeological evidence; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the same parallel-witness role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

=== The chapter's place in the typology and the comparative reading

The Coatepec narrative is in the present book's typology a cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat — that is, a combat that does both kinds of order-making work simultaneously rather than performing a single one — with the cosmogonic work consisting in the establishment of the proper celestial order (the regnant sun above the defeated moon and stars) and the sovereignty work consisting in Huitzilopochtli's establishment as the patron deity of the Mexica state and the legitimating reference for the polity's political-ritual order.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cultural_relevance.function entries on royal-legitimation and cosmological functions; scope.md §"Subject definition" 1 (cosmogonic) and 2 (sovereignty / kingship) on the two order-making subtypes that the Coatepec narrative simultaneously instantiates; Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the simultaneous cosmogonic and sovereignty work of the combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The chapter's relation to the post-conquest filtering of the source-record is methodologically central: every textual claim about the Coatepec narrative in the present chapter is built on post-conquest Nahuatl-and-Spanish texts compiled in a context of active suppression of Mexica narrative-religion, and the chapter's reading of the narrative as a combat-cosmogony — and as an unambiguous one — must be held alongside the recognition that the texts on which the reading depends were shaped by Spanish friars whose conceptual frames included pre-existing notions of "war in heaven" derived from biblical-and-medieval Christian sources, with the consequent risk of category-import.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the methodological centrality of the post-conquest filtering of the source-record, on the consequent risk of category-import from biblical-and-medieval Christian "war in heaven" notions in the Spanish-friar redaction of the Mexica narrative, and on the methodological discipline of holding the combat-cosmogony reading alongside this filtering recognition; Matos Moctezuma 1988, interpretive chapters on the same methodological situation; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml lacunae entry on the post-conquest character of the textual record.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

[INFERENCE: the chapter's combat-cosmogony reading of the Coatepec narrative is robust against the post-conquest-filtering risk because the architectural and iconographic evidence at the Templo Mayor — recovered archaeologically and independent of the Spanish-friar textual redaction — supplies a parallel non-textual witness that confirms the broad outlines of the narrative as preserved in the *Florentine Codex* Book 3, with the Coyolxauhqui stone, the serpent-headed balustrades, and the structural layout of the double pyramid being pre-conquest Mexica artefacts that pre-date the post-conquest textual redaction. | basis: Matos Moctezuma 1988's argument that the architectural and iconographic evidence at the Templo Mayor is independent of the post-conquest textual redaction and supplies a parallel non-textual witness; the Coyolxauhqui stone discovered in 1978 dates to the Stage IV phase of the Templo Mayor construction (mid-fifteenth century CE under the rulers Moctezuma I and Axayácatl) and pre-dates the Spanish conquest by approximately seven decades; the architectural design of the double pyramid is documented in the pre-conquest Mexica record. | risk: the iconographic interpretation of the Coyolxauhqui stone and the architectural identification of the Templo Mayor as Coatepec are themselves post-conquest scholarly readings, dependent on the post-conquest textual record for the identification of the figures and the narrative; the parallel-witness argument therefore reduces but does not eliminate the post-conquest-filtering risk, and the chapter holds the combat-cosmogony reading as a defensible scholarly synthesis under continuous methodological monitoring rather than as an entirely text-independent fact.]

The Coatepec narrative's place in the wider chaoskampf typology of the present book is therefore — when held alongside the methodological caveats — among the strongest single cases of cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat outside the Eurasian heartland of the chaoskampf tradition: the narrative is independently attested in textual, architectural, iconographic, and ritual evidence; it does cosmogonic and sovereignty work simultaneously; and the architectural-embedding at the political-ritual centre of a state polity is unparalleled in explicitness.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cultural_relevance.centrality entry on the central status of the Coatepec narrative within Mexica state religion; Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, treatment of non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels on the Coatepec narrative as one of the strongest single cases outside the Eurasian heartland of the chaoskampf tradition; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the unparalleled architectural-embedding at the political-ritual centre of a state polity.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony), where Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec joins Marduk in the *Enūma Eliš* (chapter 3), Ninurta in *Lugal-e* (chapter 1), and Gonggong in the *Huainanzi* (chapter 21) as one of the four strongest independent attestations of the cosmogonic-combat subtype across non-cognate traditions, with the Coatepec narrative's architectural-embedded character being its distinctive contribution to that comparative argument.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks entry on Part II C1's pairing of Huitzilopochtli with Marduk, Ninurta, and Gonggong as the four strongest independent attestations of the cosmogonic-combat subtype; scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C1 (Combat as cosmogony — Marduk, Ninurta/Asag, Gonggong, Huitzilopochtli); Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the Coatepec narrative's architectural-embedded character as its distinctive contribution.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter's principal back-link within the present book is to chapter 21 on Gonggong, with which the present chapter shares the cosmic-mountain motif as architectural-and-cosmological anchor of the combat: Gonggong's broken Mount Buzhou is the cosmic pillar whose striking tilts the cosmos, and the Templo Mayor as Coatepec is the cosmic mountain whose summit is the place of the combat — both narratives use a named mountain as the architectural-and-cosmological anchor, although Gonggong's Mount Buzhou is broken in the combat and Templo Mayor's Coatepec is built up to enact the combat.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cross_cultural_parallels entry on the cosmic-mountain parallel between Templo Mayor (= Coatepec) and Gonggong's Mount Buzhou (broken pillar); Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the cosmic-mountain motif at the Templo Mayor; scope.md §"Book structure" cluster 9 on the Gonggong chapter (chapter 21).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters

The chapter's secondary forward-link is to the next chapter (chapter 25, the family chapter on other Mesoamerican combats), within which the Cipactli dismemberment-cosmogony marks the present book's boundary between in-scope chaoskampf and out-of-scope dismemberment-cosmogony per scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" — the Mesoamerican tradition itself containing both types and supplying the within-tradition contrast that the next chapter develops.footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks entry on the Cipactli dismemberment-cosmogony in the chapter 25 family marking the book's boundary; scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs" 1 on dismemberment cosmogonies without combat as out-of-scope; López Austin 1993 on the Mesoamerican within-tradition contrast between combat-cosmogony (Huitzilopochtli) and dismemberment-without-combat cosmogony (Cipactli).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat

The chapter's relation to the methods sidebar at chapter 33 (between Part I and Part II of the present book) is load-bearing on two methodological points: the area-specialist citation policy (Matos Moctezuma 1988, Carrasco 1999, López Austin 1993 cited by name in the first factual pass per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol") and the post-conquest textual filtering issue (which the chapter handles via the [LACUNA] marker in the source overview and via continuous methodological registration in the body claims).footnote:[Per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml special_instructions on the area-specialist citation policy applying to the chapter; scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement for non-Eurasian-tradition chapters; chapter 33 methods sidebar (per toc.yaml) as the chapter that articulates the appropriation protocol and the area-specialist policy at full length.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

The Coatepec narrative's cultural-historical importance within the Mesoamerican tradition is — alongside the Cipactli cosmogony, the Quetzalcoatl-Tezcatlipoca alternation across the Five Suns cycle, and the foundation narrative of Tenochtitlan from the wandering of the Mexica from Aztlán — one of the load-bearing narratives of the Mexica state's self-understanding, and remains a central reference within Mexican cultural-and-political memory from the conquest onward into the present day, with the Coyolxauhqui stone's 1978 discovery and the subsequent Templo Mayor excavations being a major event in twentieth-century Mexican archaeology and public-cultural life.footnote:[Carrasco 1999, ch. on Coatepec, on the cultural-historical importance of the Coatepec narrative within the Mesoamerican tradition and its place alongside the Cipactli cosmogony, the Quetzalcoatl-Tezcatlipoca alternation across the Five Suns cycle, and the foundation narrative of Tenochtitlan; Matos Moctezuma 1988 on the 1978 Coyolxauhqui stone discovery and the subsequent Templo Mayor excavations as a major event in twentieth-century Mexican archaeology and public-cultural life; López Austin 1993, chapter on cosmic combat, on the same cultural-historical importance.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Marduk-Tiamat, Ch. 3) — cosmogonic-combat parallel: like Marduk's defeat of Tiamat, Huitzilopochtli's defeat of Coyolxauhqui at the moment of his birth establishes the proper celestial order; both narratives use a single decisive cosmogonic combat; both involve a young god emerging to defeat a generationally-prior or sibling-rank divine-order figure; the architectural-embedded character of Coatepec at the Templo Mayor is the distinctive Mexica contribution to this parallel; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cross_cultural_parallels (Mesopotamia / Marduk) and comparative_hooks (Part II C1).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesopotamia (Ninurta-Asag, Ch. 1) — cosmogonic-combat parallel within the four-cases set for Part II C1; Ninurta's combat with Asag in Lugal-e and Huitzilopochtli's combat with Coyolxauhqui at Coatepec share the cosmogonic order-making function; the four-cases set (Marduk, Ninurta, Gonggong, Huitzilopochtli) supplies Part II C1 with four strongest independent attestations across non-cognate traditions.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: China (Gonggong, Ch. 21) — cosmic-mountain parallel: both Gonggong's Mount Buzhou (broken pillar at the cosmic centre) and Templo Mayor's Coatepec (cosmic mountain at the political-ritual centre) use a named mountain as the architectural-and-cosmological anchor of the combat; the difference is structural — Mount Buzhou is broken in the combat (its destruction tilts the cosmos), Coatepec is built in stone to enact the combat (its construction perpetuates the order); per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cross_cultural_parallels (China / Gonggong).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Hebrew Bible / Christian (Revelation 12, Ch. 9) — god-emerges-from-womb-and-defeats-adversary structural parallel: the *Florentine Codex* Book 3 narrative of Huitzilopochtli's birth-and-combat at Coatepec has been compared structurally to Revelation 12's heavenly woman, male child, and red dragon, with a young divine figure emerging from a divine mother to defeat an adversarial figure — though no transmission is claimed and the parallel is structural-typological rather than historical; the post-conquest filtering of the Mexica textual record means that the Spanish-friar redaction may have shaped the narrative in directions consonant with biblical-and-medieval Christian "war in heaven" notions, a methodological caveat that Carrasco 1999 develops; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml cross_cultural_parallels (Hebrew Bible / Christian).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mesoamerica (Cipactli dismemberment-cosmogony, Ch. 25 family) — within-tradition contrast: the same Mesoamerican mythological tradition contains both combat-cosmogony (Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec) and dismemberment-without-combat cosmogony (Cipactli torn apart by Quetzalcoatl and Tezcatlipoca to form sky and earth), supplying the present book with a within-tradition exemplar of the boundary between in-scope chaoskampf and out-of-scope dismemberment-cosmogony per scope.md §"Excluded adjacent motifs"; the next chapter develops this contrast.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Comparative ritual — architectural-embedded combat as unparalleled in explicitness: in no other chapter of the present book is the combat-narrative literally built in stone at the political-ritual centre of the relevant polity, with the architecture itself functioning as the primary statement of the combat-cosmogony; the closest parallel within the book is the Akitu festival's reading of the *Enūma Eliš* in chapter 3, but even there the festival re-enacts the narrative without the architectural-embedded character of the Templo Mayor as Coatepec; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks (Comparative ritual).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C1 (combat as cosmogony) — Huitzilopochtli at Coatepec is one of the four strongest independent attestations of the cosmogonic-combat subtype, alongside Marduk-Tiamat (Ch. 3), Ninurta-Asag (Ch. 1), and Gonggong's broken pillar (Ch. 21); the Coatepec narrative's distinctive contribution to the four-cases set is the architectural-embedded character of the combat at the Templo Mayor; per brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml comparative_hooks (Part II C1).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Part II C2 (combat as sovereignty) — the Coatepec combat does sovereignty work alongside cosmogonic work, establishing Huitzilopochtli's sovereignty over his lunar-and-stellar siblings and (by extension) the Mexica state's political authority; the narrative is therefore a secondary case for Part II C2 (which is principally anchored in Baal/Yamm, Zeus/Typhon, Indra/Vṛtra, Thraētaona, Kumarbi/Ullikummi, and Ninurta/Anzû) as a non-Eurasian comparandum.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Methods sidebar (Ch. 33) — the post-conquest textual filtering of the Mexica narrative material at Coatepec is a load-bearing test case for the methods sidebar's discussion of area-specialist citation policy, source-record-criticism, and the appropriation protocol; the chapter's [LACUNA] marker in the source overview and the [INFERENCE] markers throughout the body register the methodological situation in a form that the methods sidebar can foreground when articulating the book's general handling of post-conquest source-records and Spanish-friar redaction.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: Mexica solar-warrior cult and the Five Suns cycle — Huitzilopochtli's identity as sun-warrior god is in the Mexica theological tradition embedded within the Five Suns cycle (in which the present sun, Nahui Ollin or Tonatiuh, is the fifth in a sequence of cosmic ages); the Coatepec narrative is one of the principal Mexica narratives of the present sun's establishment, and the next family chapter on other Mesoamerican combats develops the Five Suns cycle in connection with the Quetzalcoatl-Tezcatlipoca alternation; this connection is registered here for the comparative chapter that may take it up.
```
