      Panquetzaliztli as 'the Mexica winter-solstice festival' with 'European-calendar dates
      fall around the winter solstice in late November to mid-December in the Julian system
      used in the early colonial sources.' The Panquetzaliztli veintena in the Mexica
      365-day calendar corresponds roughly to late November to mid-December in the Julian
      calendar used by 16th-century Spanish sources — consistent with Carrasco 1999. However,
      the Mexica calendar was not keyed to the solar year in the way the chapter implies:
      there was no leap-year correction, so the veintena drifted over centuries. The
      chapter's own qualifier ('with the precise correspondence varying with the Mexica
      calendrical drift') registers this correctly. PASS.

    location: >
      §"Source overview" lines 60-61 (body claim anchored source_id=forsyth-1987) and
      §"The chapter's place in the typology and the comparative reading" lines 203-204
      (body claim anchored source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=on non-Eurasian combat-myth
      parallels). Both EVIDENCE tokens at lines 61 and 204 share the same mismatch.
    claim_text: >
      (Line 60-61) "The chapter's secondary scholarly anchor for the wider non-Eurasian
      combat-myth comparison is Neil Forsyth's The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth
      (Princeton University Press, 1987), whose treatment of non-Eurasian combat-myth
      parallels supplies a comparative anchor for the Coatepec narrative's place alongside
      Mesopotamian and other combat-myths in the long-arc combat-myth tradition that the
      present book surveys."
      (Line 203-204) "...Forsyth 1987, The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth, treatment
      of non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels on the Coatepec narrative as one of the strongest
      single cases outside the Eurasian heartland of the chaoskampf tradition..."
    problem: >
      Forsyth 1987 (The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth) is, per the sources.yaml
      entry and per trained knowledge of the volume, a study that traces the combat myth
      from Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic: its coverage runs Sumerian →
      Babylonian → Hebrew Bible → intertestamental Jewish → early Christian, with primary
      focus on the Near Eastern to Judeo-Christian transmission chain. The book does not
      contain substantive chapters on Mesoamerican material. Its coverage of
      'non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels' is therefore not accurately characterized
      by the loc supplied. The chapter's claim that Forsyth supplies 'treatment of
      non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels' is an overstatement of the book's scope.
      This is the same pattern flagged in previous Ch20/21 audits (Forsyth cited for
      Aztec material it does not substantively cover), though the present use is less
      severe: both EVIDENCE tokens here are for long-arc framing claims (situating
      Coatepec within the wider combat-myth tradition the book surveys) rather than for
      Aztec-specific narrative content. The 'non-Eurasian parallels' loc characterization
      is inaccurate regardless.
      Note: the brief (24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml secondary entry) already records
      the loc as 'on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels' — so the claims document
      faithfully transmits the brief's loc characterization. The brief's loc is itself
      the source of the inaccuracy.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml entry for forsyth-1987: "Traces the combat myth from Sumer through
      Judeo-Christian apocalyptic. Full catalog-level treatment." No mention of Mesoamerican
      or non-Eurasian coverage. Trained knowledge of Forsyth 1987: the book's chapter
      structure runs through Mesopotamian, Ugaritic, Hebrew Bible, early Jewish, and
      early Christian combat-myth material; it does not include chapters on Aztec, Andean,
      Chinese, or other non-Eurasian combat traditions. The characterization 'treatment
      of non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels' is therefore not supported by the source's
      actual scope.
    recommended_fix: >
      In both EVIDENCE-bearing body claims (lines 60-61 and 203-204), replace the loc
      'on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels' with language that accurately characterizes
      Forsyth's actual scope: e.g., 'as the book's long-arc Sumer-through-Christian
      combat-myth framing anchor, used here for contextual placement of the Coatepec
      narrative within the wider combat-myth tradition the present book surveys — not
      for Aztec-specific content coverage.' The brief's secondary entry for forsyth-1987
      should likewise be corrected: the loc 'on non-Eurasian combat-myth parallels'
      should be replaced with 'as long-arc framing anchor (Sumer through Christian
      apocalyptic); cited for contextual positioning of the Coatepec narrative within
      the book's wider survey, not for Aztec-specific coverage.' The body claims'
      substance (that Coatepec is one of the strongest non-Eurasian cases in the wider
      combat-myth tradition) is sound as an editorial judgement; only the characterization
      of Forsyth as the source for that judgement requires adjustment.

  - id: FC-02
    severity: MEDIUM
    category: citation_wrong
    location: >
      §"Variants in the kinship and ritual contexts" — the body claim at line 184-185
      on Durán's Historia de las Indias de Nueva España (EVIDENCE token: source_id=
      lopez-austin-myths ; loc=The Myths of the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat). The
      same claim at line 185 in the footnote credits López Austin 1993 as the anchor
      for Durán's specific ritual-apparatus content.
    claim_text: >
      "Diego Durán's Historia de las Indias de Nueva España (compiled c. 1581 from Nahua
      informants and earlier Mexica pictorial sources, preserved in the manuscript at the
      Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid) supplies the Coatepec narrative within an
      unusually full ritual-context apparatus that ties the narrative closely to the
      Panquetzaliztli winter-solstice festival, supplying ritual-aetiological elements
      (the dough-image of the god, the mock combats of the festival, the casting of victims
      down the staircase) that the Florentine Codex Book 3 omits or treats more cursorily."
    problem: >
      The EVIDENCE token for this claim cites source_id=lopez-austin-myths (The Myths of
      the Opossum, ch. on cosmic combat) as the anchor. However, the specific claim being
      made is about what Durán's Historia de las Indias de Nueva España contains — namely,
      the dough-image (tzoalli), mock combats of the Panquetzaliztli festival, and the
      victim-casting ritual sequence. López Austin 1993 (The Myths of the Opossum) is a
      Mesoamerican comparative mythology framework that may reference Durán in passing, but
      it is not the primary authority on what Durán's Historia specifically supplies vis-à-vis
      the Panquetzaliztli ritual apparatus.
      Carrasco 1999 (City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec) is the established scholarly
      anchor for precisely this content: Carrasco 1999 explicitly treats the Panquetzaliztli
      festival sequence including the tzoalli dough-image, the mock combats between warriors
      representing Huitzilopochtli's forces and the Centzon Huitznahua, and the casting of
      victims onto the Coyolxauhqui stone. The claim at line 92-93 of the same chapter
      correctly anchors the Panquetzaliztli festival sequence description to carrasco-1999-city.
      The Durán-specific claim at line 184-185 should share the same anchor (or add
      carrasco-1999-city alongside lopez-austin-myths), not route through López Austin alone.
    evidence: >
      The claims document at lines 92-93 correctly cites carrasco-1999-city for the
      Panquetzaliztli festival sequence including 'the dough-image (tzoalli) of Huitzilopochtli
      processed through the city, the mock combats between pairs of warriors representing
      Huitzilopochtli's forces and the Centzon Huitznahua, the casting of sacrificial victims
      onto the Coyolxauhqui stone.' The identical content is claimed at line 184-185 to come
      from Durán's Historia, but is anchored to López Austin 1993 rather than to Carrasco 1999
      — creating an inconsistency in which the same ritual-apparatus content is correctly
      attributed to Carrasco at one point and incorrectly attributed to López Austin at another.
      López Austin 1993's ch. on cosmic combat focuses on the Mesoamerican cosmogonic-combat
      typology; it does not function as the standard authority for Durán's Panquetzaliztli
      ritual-apparatus content.
    recommended_fix: >
      At line 184-185, change the EVIDENCE token from source_id=lopez-austin-myths to
      source_id=carrasco-1999-city ; loc=City of Sacrifice, ch. on Coatepec, with the
      footnote citing Carrasco 1999 as the anchor for Durán's ritual-context material
      and López Austin 1993 as the Mesoamerican-comparative framing source. The brief's
      variants.alternates entry for Durán's Historia credits it to lopez-austin-myths,
      which may have propagated the misrouting; the brief should be updated to attribute
      the Durán ritual-context variant description to Carrasco 1999 rather than to López
      Austin 1993.

  - id: FC-03
    severity: LOW
    category: unverified
    location: >
      §"Source overview" — the VARIANT marker at line 120 names "(Sahagún 1545-1590;
      Anderson-Dibble English translation)" as the primary version of the Florentine Codex
      Book 3 narrative on which the chapter's prevalent version is based.
    claim_text: >
      "primary=Florentine Codex Book 3 (Sahagún 1545-1590; Anderson-Dibble English
      translation): Coatlicue is impregnated by a ball of feathers..."
    problem: >
      The Anderson-Dibble English translation (Arthur J.O. Anderson and Charles E. Dibble,
      trans., Florentine Codex: General History of the Things of New Spain, 13 vols.,
      University of Utah Press / School of American Research, 1950-1982) is the standard
      scholarly English translation of the Florentine Codex and the de facto translation
      the chapter's narrative-detail claims are filtered through. However, Anderson-Dibble
      does not appear in sources.yaml. The EVIDENCE tokens for all Florentine Codex narrative
      claims route through matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor and carrasco-1999-city (which discuss
      the Florentine Codex content as secondary interpreters), rather than through Anderson-
      Dibble as the primary translation. This means no body claim formally lacks a sources.yaml
      anchor — but the VARIANT marker's naming of Anderson-Dibble as the primary version
      implies the chapter draft will quote or paraphrase Anderson-Dibble, creating a citation
      that the source registry cannot handle at draft stage.
      This is a pipeline-management issue rather than a factual error: the body claims are
      correctly routed through sources.yaml entries. But the absence of Anderson-Dibble from
      sources.yaml will need to be remedied before the chapter-draft stage, when narrative
      prose paraphrasing the Florentine Codex Book 3 will require a page-cited anchor in the
      primary translation.
    evidence: >
      sources.yaml: no entry with id=anderson-dibble or similar. The VARIANT marker at line
      120 names the translation explicitly. The narratological detail claims (ball of feathers,
      Cuahuitlicac's role, dismemberment sequence, *xiuhcōātl* description) in §"The Coatepec
      narrative" and §"The combat at Coatepec" are claims about the Florentine Codex Book 3
      text that will need page-level citations in the chapter draft. Without Anderson-Dibble
      in sources.yaml, the chapter draft cannot supply those citations in a form the pipeline
      can validate.
    recommended_fix: >
      Add an Anderson-Dibble entry to sources.yaml before the chapter-draft stage:
        id: anderson-dibble-florentine
        source_tier: primary-translation
        author: "Anderson, Arthur J.O.; Dibble, Charles E."
        title: "Florentine Codex: General History of the Things of New Spain"
        year: 1950-1982
        full_citation: "Sahagún, Bernardino de. Florentine Codex: General History of the Things
          of New Spain, trans. A.J.O. Anderson and C.E. Dibble, 13 vols. (Santa Fe: School
          of American Research / Salt Lake City: University of Utah, 1950-1982)."
        short_citation: "Anderson-Dibble 1950-82"
        notes: "Standard scholarly English translation of the Nahuatl Florentine Codex. Book 3
          ('The Origin of the Gods') contains the fullest narrative account of Huitzilopochtli's
          birth at Coatepec and the killing of Coyolxauhqui and the Centzon Huitznahua."
      Once added, the Florentine Codex narrative-detail EVIDENCE tokens in §"The Coatepec
      narrative" and §"The combat at Coatepec" should include anderson-dibble-florentine
      as the co-anchor (alongside matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor as interpretive secondary).
      This is a LOW finding at the claims stage because the current routing is pipeline-
      valid; the issue will become HIGH at the draft stage if not remedied.

  - id: FC-04
    severity: NOTE
    category: note
    location: >
      Multiple EVIDENCE tokens across §"Source overview" and §"The Mexica state, the
      festival, and the architecture" anchored source_id=matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor
      ; loc=The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive chapters.
    claim_text: >
      (Representative) "The chapter's principal scholarly anchor for the archaeological-
      iconographic dimension is Eduardo Matos Moctezuma's The Great Temple of the Aztecs:
      Treasures of Tenochtitlan (Thames and Hudson, 1988), the standard English-language
      interpretive monograph by the archaeologist who directed the Templo Mayor excavations
      from 1978 onward..."
    problem: >
      The loc 'interpretive chapters' is used throughout for Matos Moctezuma 1988 — 28
      of the 57 EVIDENCE tokens specify this loc. No chapter titles, chapter numbers, or
      page ranges are supplied. This creates a consistent but vague citation structure
      in which virtually all architectural, iconographic, and narrative-detail claims in
      the chapter are anchored at the same vague loc level. This is consistent with the
      brief's specification for Matos Moctezuma 1988 (the brief says 'The Great Temple of
      the Aztecs, interpretive chapters — Coatepec as Templo Mayor, archaeological and
      iconographic evidence') — so the claims document faithfully transmits the brief's
      citation level. But the vagueness will require resolution before the chapter draft.
      This is a NOTE rather than a finding: no claim is wrong, no claim lacks a
      sources.yaml anchor, and no claim is unsupported by the source. The issue is
      citation-specificity depth, which the brief itself does not supply.
    evidence: >
      Brief 24-huitzilopochtli-coatepec.yaml, sources.primary entry for
      matos-moctezuma-templo-mayor: loc = 'The Great Temple of the Aztecs, interpretive
      chapters — Coatepec as Templo Mayor, archaeological and iconographic evidence.'
      No chapter numbers given. Contrast: brief 21-gonggong.yaml specifies Lewis 2006
      at 'chs. 2-3.' The asymmetry in brief-level citation discipline between the
      Chinese chapters (chapter-number precision) and the Mesoamerican chapter (vague
      'interpretive chapters') is a NOTE for the brief-maintainer.
    recommended_fix: >
      Before the chapter-draft stage, open Matos Moctezuma 1988 and identify the specific
      chapters that cover: (1) the Templo Mayor architectural identification as Coatepec,
      (2) the Coyolxauhqui stone discovery and iconography, (3) the Florentine Codex Book 3
      narrative in the context of the Templo Mayor programme, (4) the Panquetzaliztli
      festival in its architectural context, (5) the seven building stages. Update the brief
      with specific chapter titles and page ranges so that the chapter draft can supply
      pinned citations. This would allow the 28 matos-moctezuma EVIDENCE tokens to be
      validated at the page level rather than the 'interpretive chapters' vague level.

    verbatim_quote: >

      Moctezuma II) as the major expansionist rulers of the Triple Alliance state and
      the broad Pacific-to-Gulf geographic scope of the empire at its height are
      confirmed per trained knowledge.

  - claim_number: 20
    claim: >
      The Coatepec narrative is typologized as a cosmogonic-and-sovereignty combat —
      simultaneously performing cosmogonic work (establishing the proper celestial order)
      and sovereignty work (establishing Huitzilopochtli's sovereignty over the Mexica
      pantheon and (by extension) the Mexica state's political-ritual order).

# Input file: briefs/23-susanoo-orochi.yaml

```
chapter_number: 23
slug: "susanoo-orochi"
title: "Susanoo vs. Yamata no Orochi (Chaoskampf as Applied Label)"
chapter_anchor: "ch-23-susanoo-orochi"

chapter_type: "related (full, with explicit outside-framing marker)"

sources:
  primary:
    - id: philippi-kojiki
      loc: "Kojiki 1.19–1.20 (Philippi 1968 translation)"
      identifier: "ISBN 9780691611358"
    - id: naumann-mythen
      loc: "Die Mythen des alten Japan (Orochi chapter) — metallurgical-allegory reading"
      identifier: "ISBN 9783406421648"
  secondary:
    - { id: forsyth-1987, loc: "on East Asian combat-myth" }

variants:
  classification: co-equal
  summary: |
    The Kojiki (712 CE) provides a single coherent account. The Nihon Shoki (720 CE)
    juxtaposes multiple "one writing says" variants, presenting the story as a set of
    co-equal versions. The chapter treats both imperial chronicles as the primary textual
    base and presents their divergences where relevant.

summary: |
  Expelled from heaven for his misdeeds, Susanoo descends to Izumo, where the eight-headed
  serpent Yamata no Orochi has eaten seven of an elderly couple's eight daughters and is
  coming for the eighth. Susanoo intoxicates the serpent with eight vats of sake, dismembers
  it, and finds the sword Kusanagi ("grass-cutter") in its tail — a founding regalium of the
  imperial line.

cultural_relevance:
  centrality: central (as imperial-founding narrative)
  function: |
    - sword-etiology (Kusanagi as one of the Three Sacred Treasures)
    - royal-legitimation (imperial line's mythic charter)
    - per Naumann / Isomae / Como / Ooms: metallurgical allegory — the serpent represents
      iron-smelting and the sword's emergence from its body represents the craft of
      sword-making
  cross_cultural_parallels: |
    - On COMPARATIVIST readings: parallels Indra-Vṛtra and Thor-Jörmungandr as storm-god /
      serpent combat.
    - On AREA-SPECIALIST readings: the label 'chaoskampf' is applied from outside; the
      story's internal logic is sword-etiology, not chaos-ordering.

target_length_words: 5500

special_instructions: |
  CRITICAL: Per scope.md §"Book structure" and audit-review-log.md, this chapter is
  DEMOTED FROM CORE precisely because Japanese specialists (Naumann, Isomae, Como, Ooms)
  frame the story as sword-etiology / metallurgical allegory, not chaoskampf. The chapter
  MUST treat the label as applied from outside. Open with the Japanese framing (imperial
  regalium, metallurgical origin, sacred-treasure charter). The comparative chaoskampf
  reading is presented as a 20th-century-Western move that the chapter engages with but
  does not endorse. Area-specialist citation policy applies: cite Naumann, Isomae, Como,
  and Ooms by name in the first factual pass, per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol". The
  chapter feeds Part II C7 (inversion / transformation) and C8 (refusal and appropriation)
  — it is a working example of the book's willingness to hold the comparative category
  loosely where area-specialists require it.
```
